B006QVRVJK|5.0|\\"Carnage\\" is an adaptation of Yasmina Reza's original stage play \\"Le Dieu du carnage (God of Carnage)\\" for the big screen, written and directed by Roman Polanski, in a translation from the original French by Michael Katims. Owing to Polanski's fugitive status in the USA, it was filmed (despite its Brooklyn setting) in Paris.<br /><br />The film makes excellent use of minimal set additions to the original stage production and the four cast members are all outstanding in their performances. The story itself is a comedy of manners, as two sets of parents to teenage boys meet in an attempt to resolve matters arising from a playground scrap between their respective sons.  The script explores the basis and boundaries of politeness versus honesty, social conscience versus self-interest and portrays the disastrous consequences of testing those boundaries. The excruciating shallow play-acting of social correctness is gradually replaced by the increasingly painful exposition of the truth as social hypocrisies are systematically dissected to display the selfish core.<br /><br />Throughout the film's 80-minute duration, allegiances and alliances shift, battle lines are drawn and redrawn, as prejudices, obsessions, fears and foibles are successively exposed, probed and ridiculed, emotions running high as opinions and values are successively attacked and defended; and the opening tensions are dissipated through farce.<br /><br />The script sticks closely to the original stage version, varying only in providing a neat storyline twist using the opening and closing title visuals, which poignantly shows up both the futility of the parents' attempts to resolve issues through their own \\"grown-up and adult\\" discussions, as well as the fact that all are equally at fault. The humour is subtle in its steps but hilarious in its cumulative effect. Those who know the play may be disappointed with Polanski's direction, which gives a less intimate but more claustrophobic feel to the enterprise. The film is nevertheless first rate and is heartily recommended.The film is nevertheless first rate and is heartily recommended.
0439023513|5.0|After reading the first two books of the series, I thought I had a pretty good idea of how the final book would turn out--a lot of exciting action scenes, some moral ambiguity, but ultimately good triumphs over evil.  I'm sure most of the book's fans (and certainly those who tuned in after seeing the movie) expected something similar.  And a book like that would have been a fitting ending, generally satisfying to most people.<br /><br />But, surprise! surprise!--Suzanne Collins decided not to go in that direction.  Instead she wrote one of the most human and moving books in this sort of genre that I have ever read.  At the end I was crying for Katniss, and Prim, and the cat, and Peeta, and Finnick, and Haymitch and the other characters I had come to love, and whose lives were scarred by the powerful forces that had used them as pawns in their game.  Collins gives us a real taste of the impact of war on the individual humans that are thrown into it.  The Hunger Games in this book is not of 24 individuals fighting as entertainment for the home viewing audience, it is tens of thousands of people fighting to serve the personal ambitions of a few powerful people at the top of the pyramid.  The fighting is not the central point of this book--we know how that is going to turn out.  The point is what happens to the people--and Collins doesn't take the easy way out on this front; we're presented with a lot of very difficult, very sad and very real consequences that just took my breath away.<br /><br />I loved this book.

B007CKNX28|5.0|If Jack White hasn't created a masterpiece in \\"Blunderbuss,\\" he's come damn close. Proving he has quite a future post-White Stripes, he has cobbled together a collection of tunes that pulse, soothe and always rock, flying by in a slick, fast-moving fashion. It marks one of those rare occasions where a mental and spiritual exorcism for the artist is also a destined pleasure for those interested in absorbing his art.<br /><br />Unpretentious, callow, straightforward, laid-back - these are hardly attributes that applied to White previously, and now that he is relying solely on his own instincts and proclivities this is even more pronounced. Oftentimes he veers up and down the emotional scale several times within the same song, taking the listener in unexpected directions that provide a roadmap of both his disillusionments and idiosyncrasies.<br /><br />To be sure, White is a restless presence on \\"Blunderbuss\\" - lamenters of the demise of White Stripes will find a musical life boat within its gripping, extremely impressive 42 minutes. Thematic comparison to Dylan's \\"Blood on the Tracks\\" is certainly justified. This is an album of one man's heartbreak and the turmoil, introspection and eventual resurrection which followed.<br /><br />His elegant, rapturous playing on his Rhodes and his grand, aggressive guitar playing bolster the tunes with a full-bodied precision that is by now expected. The extremely catchy \\"Freedom at 21,\\" which features a fiery, inspired near-rap, is illustrative of the latter, while the schizophrenic, relatively quiet \\"Hypocritical Kiss\\" exemplifies the former.<br /><br />This is music that not only compels repeated plays but reveals further treasures with them. There is an immediacy to White's often unsettling vocal performances, as though he walked into the recording booth to lay down the tracks immediately after tweaking the final melody lines. He minces no words and purges his emotions unrepentantly. This works since the swelling, often riotous music surges forth to sync up with his dour sentiments.<br /><br />\\"I want love to grab my fingers slowly, slam them in a doorway, put my face in the ground\\" from \\"Love Interruption\\" is characteristic of his self-effacement. It seems he will not attend any speed-dating sessions in the near future. As a matter of fact, his wrenchingly negative opinion of women (and love in general) is made so clear that it would not be altogether surprising were NOW to issue a formal response to the album.<br /><br />Most important is the vintage of the music, however, and Jack White proves himself to be an incredible musician here. He is a fascinating and frustratingly enigmatic talent who consistently makes choices that are not merely unorthodox but downright original, all the while expressing himself in a way that feels familiar - as though the sounds and sentiments he puts forth were already confirmed truths in search of a mouthpiece to report them to the world. That quality of conviction is rare and only part of what makes \\"Blunderbuss\\" a must-listen.music surges forth to sync up with his dour sentiments.<br /><br />\\"I want love to grab my fingers slowly, slam them in a doorway, put my face in the ground\\" from \\"Love Interruption\\" is characteristic of his self-effacement. He is a fascinating and frustratingly enigmatic talent who consistently makes choices that are not merely unorthodox but downright original, all the while expressing himself in a way that feels familiar - as though the sounds and sentiments he puts forth were already confirmed truths in search of a mouthpiece to report them to the world. That quality of conviction is rare and only part of what makes \\"Blunderbuss\\" a must-listen.

B007CKNX28|5.0|What is it about Jack White? He blazes a trail where others follow, he captures the moment and he is always ten yards in the lead. On \\"Blunderbuss\\" his debut solo album all elements of White's recent past are to found with recognizable snatches of the White Stripes, The Raconteurs and his slightly less successful recent outing The Dead Weather. But White is not standing still, far from it since \\"Blunderbuss\\" is an incredibly varied album ranging from huge riff heavy hard rock anthems like \\"Sixteen Saltines\\" to the breezy jazzy pop of \\"Hip (eponymous) poor boy\\". More than this like Dylan's \\"Blood on tracks\\" the themes of break up and divorce runs through this album like a fault line and informs the lyrical preoccupations of many of the songs.<br /><br />It is true that White's split with British model Karen Elson seems amicable enough with the couple holding a party to celebrate the \\"making and breaking of the sacred union of marriage,\\" and indeed Elson does some background vocals on three of the songs here. Yet in the first half of \\"Blunderbuss\\" in particular White does rally against unhappy domestic predicaments, collapsing relationships and at one point the perfidy of women. You sense as the Americans politely put it that he has \\"issues\\" some of which are dramatically captured in the opener \\"Missing Pieces\\". While it is possibly the most White Stripeish song on the album its also has a strong confessional bent as White at one point muses that \\"When someone tells you they can't live without you, they ain't lying/ They'll take pieces of you and walk away.\\" albeit within the confines of a killer song exemplifying him at his absolute best. On \\"Freedom at 21\\" it gets even nastier with a greasy rolling riff which he must have held back from the Raconteurs and a rally against internet trolling where he stingily rebukes the perpetrator with the words \\"she don't care what kind of wounds she's inflicted on me/ she don't care what color bruises that she is leaving on me\\". It not all bitter and twisted however, far from it. The lovely title track \\"Blunderbuss\\" is almost a Robert Plant like lament by a wordy story, gorgeous piano lines and violins which conjure up echoes of another Dylan album namely \\"Desire\\". \\"Hypocritical kiss\\" is one of the best songs White has written in quite a time and it rolls along underpinned by strong melodic piano lines and shows White's complete mastery as a songwriter. It wouldn't be Jack White however without a nice blast of garage rock and nod to the Blasters cover of the Little Willie John standard \\"I'm Shakin\\" is completely nailed. Equally \\"Trash Tongue Talker\\" whilst breaking no new ground is a beautifully dismissive song dripping venom and owing a large debt James Booker. Other highlights include the dark acoustic ballad `Love Interruption' which combine White on a husky duet with Ruby Amanfu. Perhaps the two most distinct songs are kept until last not least the Beatles like dark pop of the brilliant \\"On and On\\" and finally the very funky \\"Take me with you when you go\\" which at the songs mid point breaks out in a pulsating sing-along and a fitting conclusion to an excellent album.<br /><br />Uncut has recently proclaimed \\"Blunderbuss\\" to be the obvious successor to \\"Get behind me Satan\\" the White Stripes fifth album and it is true that piano dominates as much as guitar on this album. White has also in an interview within the same magazine played down the confessional element to the album but he can be a contradictory cuss who regularly warns that many of his public statements are pure invention. What is certain is that \\"Blunderbuss\\" is a sure fire success, an album which is surly, aggressive yet calm and likeable in equal measures. White has bestrode the music scene like a colossus over the past ten years and the truly excellent \\"Blunderbuss\\" will reinforce this benevolent dictatorship.not all bitter and twisted however, far from it. 1583334386|5.0|We generally think of ourselves as one body and one mind ... a single unit working together.  But according to Kelly McGonigal, the author, \\"...we've seen again and again that we are not one self, but multiple selves.  Our human nature includes both the self that wants immediate gratification, and the self with a higher purpose.\\"  This is what makes self-control or willpower so difficult, we are actually fighting against ourselves.<br /><br />The benefits of self-control are significant.  \\"People who have better control of their attention, emotions, and actions are better off almost any way you look at it.  They are happier and healthier.  Their relationships are more satisfying and last longer.  They make more money and go further in their careers.\\"<br /><br />The Willpower Instinct is a guide for improving your self-control.  It is based on the award winning class which Ms. McGonigal teaches at Sanford University.  Her writing style is very conversational and entertaining.  She has a keen sense of humor which shows up throughout the book.  While she is a college professor, the book was written for the general public in easy to understand language.<br /><br />Many of the concepts in the book are counterintuitive.  We think the warning on cigarette packages should work to deter smoking.  They actually have the opposite effect.  \\"Frightening cigarette warnings can make smokers crave a cigarette, economic crises can make a person shop, and the nightly news can make you fat.\\"<br /><br />The book is filled with examples of how and why we have willpower failures and ways we can work around these failures.  \\"Self-control is a matter of understanding these different parts of ourselves, not fundamentally changing who we are.  In the quest for self-control, the usual weapons we wield against ourselves - guilt, stress and shame - don't work.\\"  What does work is self-awareness.  Paying attention to what is going on inside and realizing that there is a difference between thinking about something and acting on those thoughts.<br /><br />This book will give you a lot of insights into why we do the things we do, where our willpower fails us and why.  You will also find many exercises which will help you strengthen your willpower.  There is a lot of information in this book.  I doubt that you will be able to absorb all of it in one reading.  But it is very entertaining and should be studied and referred to often to help you build and strengthen your willpower.  It will have a significant impact on your life.<br /><br />Excellent resource for improving your willpower and your life.ng about something and acting on those thoughts.<br /><br />This book will give you a lot of insights into why we do the things we do, where our willpower fails us and why. 0439023513|4.0|Katniss Everdeen, the girl on fire, is about to take on a new pseudonym, the Mockingjay.  She has survived two Hunger Games but is about to face her biggest threat yet, President Snow and the Capitol.  She has joined forces with the rebels and wants to make right, all the wrongs that have been done.  Mockingjay, by Suzanne Collins, is the third instalment in The Hunger Games trilogy, bringing an end to the tale of Katniss and her fellow tribute Peeta, from district twelve.  I found this instalment to be my least favorite of the three Hunger Games books, but I would still recommend it if you want to finish the journey with the characters you have grown to love.<br /><br />The story begins with Panem in civil war; no longer are the districts happy with their oppression.  They have lived for the past seventy-five years in poverty and starvation providing the Capitol with lavish luxury.  A similar synopsis can be found in the movie Demolition Man (1993) where a utopian society called San Angeles has been made by Dr. Raymond Cocteau.  Any people not willing to live under Cocteau's rules are forced to live in the sewers, fighting to stay alive and not die of starvation.  However, this all changes when Edgar Friendly brings the sewer dwellers together and declares civil war against the pampered overseers of San Angeles.<br /><br />We learn quickly the foreshadowed district thirteen is still alive and well.  In fact, the thirteenth district once known for its uranium production has now moved underground, its inhabitance living in an intricate maze of bunkers connected by elevators that move in all directions.  I couldn`t help but think of the Fallout video games where the world has been devastated by nuclear war and the few people left on Earth now live in vaults.<br /><br />The thirteenth district has become the home of the rebels, and they push the civil war ever closer to the Capitol.  Their efforts are slow yet their decision to create a symbol of hope and power to encourage the other districts to follow suit works to their advantage.  Katniss becomes the Mockingjay, hated by the Capitol and President Snow, yet loved by the oppressed.<br /><br />I would recommend this book if you liked the first two, but I must admit I did not enjoy it as much as I had hoped.  The story is told in first person, present tense, and it is hard at times to be inside Katniss's head as she deals with all her past traumatic experiences and inner conflicts.  I found the author used a great formula when writing the first two books, but the formula seemed to change with the third installment, setting the story apart, but not in a good way.  I'd still recommend this book, but I do not hold it to the same standards as I did the first two.  You'll have to read the story to find out what happens to Peeta and Gale, but Katniss has definitely found herself in the forefront of the civil war, dressed and ready to take on the Capitol as the Mockingjay.

The piano is pretty and that's the only compliment I can say about it.  Despite all the people that believe it's a highlight, I fail to see why.  \\"In Search of Little Sadie\\" has Tom Petty-like vocals and... I can't even identify what genre this is supposed to fall under.  It morphs into blues around the 1:20 mark but before that?  What IS that?  Really cool vocals though- hey, I'm the first in line for uniqueness so no matter how it's expressed!  \\"Let It Be Me\\" is generic balladry junk.  At attempt at soul perhaps but doesn't work.\\"Little Sadie\\" is an improvement since it's a fast-moving country rocker.  Sort of filler-ish and too short, though.  \\"Woogie Boogie\\" is the *definition* of filler- a generic blues riff sets the pace for a boring instrumental, and the sax solo at the end is completely unnecessary.  \\"Belle Isle\\" is another country ballad with pointless orchestration that just increases the unnecessary amount of sappiness.<br /><br />\\"Living the Blues\\" is more short country blues.  Probably the best song on side two since at LEAST the vocals are decent.  Side two has made it perfectly clear that it doesn't have a reason to exist because this selection of songs is just really mediocre.  This version of \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" is live and of course doesn't even try to stand in the same league as the Highway 61 Revisited version thanks to the rushed, less than stellar vocal performance taking place.  Again, don't even THINK about comparing this to the superior Highway 61 Revisited version.  \\"Copper Kettle\\" is gently sung filler material with delicate female vocals in the background.  It's unmelodic too.  \\"Gotta Travel On\\" (yes, PLEASE Dylan, travel on to bigger and better places!) is a decent country song and not much different from the Allman Brothers Band classic \\"Melissa\\" (at least the two songs sound alike during the verse melody).<br /><br />Now arrives MORE questionable material.  \\"Blue Moon\\" is at least alright because Dylan puts his own unique spin on a familiar doo-wop classic (unintentionally perhaps, since the only unique thing about it is his voice).  It's slow-paced and forgettable though.  I DO love the violin at the end and how it plays the verse melody for a few seconds.  Pretty cool moment that is.  \\"The Boxer\\" is a good song that actually captures the spirit of Simon & Garfunkel's \\"The Boxer\\" but unfortunately doesn't extend the \\"la la la\\" chorus at the end so there's no emotionally devastating build-up in this version.  That's one of the reasons I think so highly of the Simon & Garfunkel version.  \\"The Mighty Quinn\\" has now been turned into a piano-rockabilly version of a classic Manfred Mann song.  Vocals sound a bit off to me.  Not just anyone can sing this particular vocal melody and Dylan obviously can't.<br /><br />\\"Take Me As I Am (Or Let Me Go)\\" is another country ballad.  Can't get into it at all.  Honestly with all the country ballads scattered throughout Self Portrait, putting a really average one like this near the end is just insulting to the fans.  \\"Take a Message to Mary\\" surprisingly has a VERY good melody that's basically a perfect pop song at its core.  \\"It Hurts Me To\\" is acoustic country that's actually pretty good.  I can't say I appreciate Dylan's country/gospel/blues on most of Self Portrait but he surprises me here.  \\"Minstrel Boy\\" has an intro where the vocals are completetely out of tune!  Uh oh... that intro serves as the chorus.  Yikes.  The chorus is honestly nothing but barely adequate drunken nonsense with a distinct lack of foresight.  We shouldn't have to hear this kind of mediocre garbage, Bob!  \\"She Belongs to Me\\" is an average version of a classic.  \\"Wigwam\\" has humming serving as the verse melody and Spanish horns in the background, but this other version I've heard with high-pitched orchestrated female vocals is better.  I actually love this song but the million does question is this REALLY want you want from Bob Dylan?  \\"Alberta #2\\" is a beautiful ballad that does a fitting job (or a tricky job, hehe) of ending a below average album.  It's highly melodic and the piano arrangements are organized tastefully.<br /><br />Overall, well the truth is, the country songs feel like leftovers from the MUCH better Nashville Skyline album, and there's lot of other mediocre songs tossed in for additional disappointment.  Only for the biggest of Dylan fans.

0143121006|4.0|Pamela Werner - vivacious only daughter of a retired British civil servant and sinophile academic - is found brutally murdered.  The colonial administration in charge at the time insists that British detectives lead the investigation. Capable local Chinese detective Colonel Han is pushed aside in favour of Chief Inspector Dennis from neighbouring Tientsin. This swiftly becomes a fatal flaw in the investigation as Colonel Han's local expertise and contacts are ignored and corruption and incompetence take over.<br /><br />The tragic tale is simply told in a straightforward manner. The period is evocatively realized and the narrow and dimly lit hutongs and gaudy unpoliced Badlands - home to pimps, prostitutes, drug addicts and other lowlife - as well as thrill-seeking expatriates - are readily visualized in the imagination through French's competent writing.<br /><br />The hero of the book, if there can be one in such a tragic story, is Pamela's father.  Denied justice by the colonial administration, he spends his time and savings hiring private detectives and agents to pursue what little evidence remains.  One can only imagine the horror as Edward Werner is forced to confront the killer regularly on the streets of Peking as the conclusive evidence to arrest him never materializes.  Edward and his daughter's killer are even interned together by the Japanese as they tighten their grip on the city.<br /><br />I met author Paul French at the launch of the paperback version in Hong Kong in September 2011 - so I am astonished it has taken so long to be released in the US.  French said he wanted the book to be an investigation of a crime and its aftermath in the same vein as Truman Capote's \\"In Cold Blood\\". As French's agent is said to have reminded him \\"You're no Truman Capote\\".  He was right. The book has its rough edges but the story and investigation are truly compelling. However, French can be satisfied in almost certainly identifying the real killer.  The only drawbackto this fascinating book is the lack of information on what became of the principal characters, especially the murder suspect.

B00782O7IY|4.0|It has been argued that art benefits from limits. Sonnets must have 14 lines and adhere to structures of rhyme and meter. Great artists don't say, \\"I can't work with those rules\\" but take up the challenge. Shakespeare wrote 154 sonnets, not including those in his plays.<br /><br />Some limitations of art can be attributed to choice and some are imposed by circumstances. The producer Val Lewton made low budget horror films in the 1940s. He didn't have the money for elaborate special effects for films like \\"Cat People\\" or \\"The Seventh Victim\\" so he had to use shadow and impressions to scare the audience. And arguably his films where the frights took place in the minds of the audience better than some films of the period that now seem dated by laughable creature effects.<br /><br />The Production Code of the Hayes Office limited what could be openly shown and talked about in mainstream Hollywood films. And though I'm not an advocate for censorship, such films as \\"Casablanca\\" and \\"Notorious\\" are much more subtle and even adult in dealing with issues of sexuality than most modern films that are free to show more body parts.<br /><br />Michel Hazanavicius, the French writer and director, of last year's acclaimed film, \\"The Artist\\", imposed upon himself several rather daunting limits. Set in Hollywood from 1927 - 32, the film, like those of the silent era has no spoken dialogue (mostly), is black and white and uses screen proportions of those years in film. Interestingly, it also complies with the content restrictions of the era (mostly; with the exception of a rude hand gesture and an implied act of violence that might not have made it to the screen at that time.)<br /><br />Jean Duardin plays a swashbuckling star of the silver screen (think Fairbank/Valentino) named George Valentin at the peak of his career who meets a young starlet on the rise (Peppy Miller played by Berenice Bejo). The advent of sound proves devastating to his career as it was for many silent stars.<br /><br />Ultimately, \\"The Artist\\" is a story about pride; a man too proud to accept help when it is needed, even to accept love.<br /><br />When his studio switches to making \\"talkies\\" exclusively, Valentin uses his own money to make an elaborate silent film that, along with the market crash, leads to his financial downfall.<br /><br />He turns out his faithful servant who desires to still serve and resists help from Peppy who desires to return the favors he granted her.<br />As Proverbs 29:23 teaches, \\"Pride brings a person low.\\" The question in the film is whether Valentin will fulfill the second part of that verse, \\"but the lowly in spirit will gain honor.\\"<br /><br />Hazanavicius playfully uses the conceits of the silent film, the facial mugging and exaggerated gestures to kid, but not mock the films of that period. He also strategically uses sound effects to hint at the great changes to come in the film world. He wisely uses the limits to push himself to make his art.<br /><br />Just as art is often improved by limits, God provides limits for his people to improve our lives. His commands are not impediments to our happiness, but like a rhyme scheme for a poem, a challenge to live richer, more creative lives for him.<br /><br />(And on a side note, if you have never taken the time to watch the great silent classics, you are missing some great fun. Comedy in particular found a unique \\"voice\\" in this era that is only hinted at in \\"The Artist\\". He also strategically uses sound effects to hint at the great changes to come in the film world. B0067EKY7M|3.0|The best thing about the film, \\"Salmon Fishing in the Yemen\\", is the imagery found in the title. The audacious idea of bringing a fishing industry to the desert evokes glorious ideas of rebirth and resurrection. But unfortunately, the soaring nature of this central theme is dragged down by dull, loathsome Hollywood clich&eacute;s.<br /><br /> Certainly, the film has an interesting pedigree. Directed by Lasse Hallstrom (who made one of the greatest of all coming of age films, \\"My Life as a Dog\\") and screenplay by Simon Beaufoy (who brings the work of a novelist to the screen as he did with \\"Slumdog Millionaire\\") gave reason for hope.<br /><br /> Ewan McGregor plays Dr. Alfred Jones. (Sidenote - I kept being distracted when people called him Dr. Jones which made me think of another famous movie scientist. Between this and playing Obi-Wan in the prequels, is he trying to redo all of George Lucas' classic characters? Howard the Duck can't be far behind. End sidenote.) Dr. Jones is an ichthyologist / British government drone challenged to help a sheik bring salmon to Yemen.<br /><br /> Emily Blunt plays Harriet, a western representative for the sheik who must persuade Jones that the project is not a boondoggle or a joke but a viable possibility. Pressure is also supplied by Kristen Scott Thomas who plays the Prime Minister's Press Secretary desperately seeking a positive story from the Middle East.<br /><br /> Thomas as the foul mouthed, devious political schemer provides some of the film's funniest moments, especially in her texting conversations with the Prime Minister, but at times her moments come across as \\"In the Loop\\" lite.<br /><br /> Another fine moment in the film comes when Jones meets with the sheik who ask if Jones is religious. Jones insists he just believes in facts and figures...He's a scientist! But the sheik counters that Jones is a fisherman and by definition that means he is a man of faith. Why else would he spend hundreds of hours waist deep in water with no assured outcome.<br /><br /> There are several interesting conversations about religion. Alfred and Harriet both admit they don't know anyone who goes to church, Alfred saying he uses Sundays to go to Target. (This is a little puzzling as before we saw Alfred with his wife practicing in a musical quartet in a church. Whatever.)<br /><br /> But as I mentioned before, tiresome clich&eacute;s emerge and to discuss them I must stoop to spoilers.<br /><br /> Though is it a spoiler to say that even though Jones is a married man and Harriet is in an affair with a soldier in Afghanistan, that the two will fall and love? For a while I thought that the message of the importance of patience and faith and fishing would be applied to the marriage of Dr. Jones. But no, because I more important rule is that in a Hollywood film the two names at the top of the bill, the pretty people, must come together. \\"Casablanca\\" is still fresh because lead characters doing what is right rather than what feels good is a rarity in films.<br /><br /> There also is lazy characterization in the film. When Blunt's boyfriend becomes inconvenient to the plot, he is shown to be a raciest, as all soldiers seem to be in Hollywood. And the sheik, we are assured, is different, a visionary who believes in peace, romance and fishing. There is talk of the clich&eacute; of \\"the magic negro\\", an African American figure whose sole purpose is to supply wisdom to the white hero. Amr Waked seems to be playing the Arab version of this role (as he did in \\"Syriana\\"). All the other Arabs seem shady and are probably all terrorists.<br /><br /> And the final annoying clich&eacute;, was the talk of \\"faith\\". In films, it is important to have faith, it doesn't matter what you put your faith in. So it's good for Alfred to put faith in something new and different like a fish hatchery in the desert and a romantic fling, but nothing so drab as an established marriage.<br /><br /> But it does, of course, matter what youput your faith in. The film has people who engage in terrorism and they certainly have faith, placed in a wrong and evil place.<br /><br /> Paul in I Corinthians 15 argues that if Christians put their faith in the resurrection and it proves untrue, then Christians are fools.<br /><br /> So faith in fishing in the Yemen, in peace and prosperity in the Middle East needs to be more than just a pretty thought one believes in, it must be possible. I wish those ideas would have been even more central in the film than another tired boy meets girl story.<br /><br />(The film is rated PG-13 for brief violence and strong language.)ilm has people who engage in terrorism and they certainly have faith, placed in a wrong and evil place.<br /><br /> Paul in I Corinthians 15 argues that if Christians put their faith in the resurrection and it proves untrue, then Christians are fools.<br /><br /> So faith in fishing in the Yemen, in peace and prosperity in the Middle East needs to be more than just a pretty thought one believes in, it must be possible. B005LAIIRQ|4.0|I hope this doesn't come as too big of a shock to you: but you're dying. Oh, it probably will take years and I certainly hope for most of us we're talking a multitude of decades. But I can only think of two people who avoided death (they're in the Bible and their names start with `E', you can look it up) and the odds are against us joining that exclusive club.<br /><br />The Grey is a film about a group of men that are facing death, likely to be sooner rather than later. A group of roughneck oil men survive a plane crash in the Alaskan wilderness but death in the form of starvation, exposure, wolf attack (you read that right) or a number of other unexpected and unpleasant possibilities seems imminent. (There's a reason the phrase \\"Feel Good Movie of the Year\\" doesn't appear on the film's lobby posters.)<br /><br />When we first meet the character played by Liam Neeson, Ottway, he is contemplating suicide for reasons that are not initially revealed. But instead he boards the doomed flight and soon finds himself fighting for his life.<br /><br />In these dire circumstances, the men must deal with the essential choices of fear and faith. One character in the film, Diaz (Frank Grillo), claims to have neither faith nor fear. Ottman calls him a fool for not being afraid in such a time, arguing it is idiocy to be fearless. Another character, Talget (Dermot Mulroney) calls him a fool for not having faith in God and a life to come.<br /><br />So is fear or fearlessness foolish? (Sorry about the excessive alliteration.) Ottman makes a very persuasive argument that fear is the most rational response to certain situations. But toward the end of the film, Ottman receives powerful arguments against fear from a couple of unexpected sources. The film seems to argue that fear is understandable, perhaps even unavoidable, (director Joe Carnahan certainly give the audience some scares), but something that must be overcome.<br /><br />For Christians, Scripture also seems to provide a schizophrenic view of fear. Proverbs 9:10 says that \\"Fear of the Lord is the beginning of wisdom.\\" Jesus argues (in Luke 12:5) for the good sense in fearing God. But note that it's just the beginning. The most common command in Scripture, spoken by angels and Jesus Himself is, \\"Don't be afraid.\\" I John 4:18 says that \\"Love drives out fear.\\"<br /><br />The Bible seems to say that fear can be a good start for assessing our situation without God's love and forgiveness. But it has no place in our lives once we have placed our faith in God's goodness. But many of the characters in \\"The Grey\\" don't believe in God, let alone a God of Mercy. They've lived brutal lives and are facing the possibility of a brutal death. Where is God in that?<br /><br />In fact, one of the characters calls out to God to show Himself (in a scene reminiscent of the cries of Job in Scripture, just much more profanity filled) and God seems to be silent.<br /><br />But is God ever fully silent?<br /><br />In Romans 1:20, the apostle Paul argues, \\"For since the creation of the world God's invisible qualities--his eternal power and divine nature--have been clearly seen, being understood from what has been made, so that people are without excuse.\\"<br /><br />The film doesn't scrimp on showing the brutality of our fallen world. But we also see much of the beauty of God's creation, not just in the majestic landscapes of the Alaskan wilderness, but also in acts of kindness and bravery from the most unlikely of human sources. Faith in God is essential to live. B005HJWWW8|5.0|I previously bought a Fiio E3 to enhance sound from my MP3 player while motorcycle riding. It's pretty good for the money, but it's also a one-trick pony. Either on or off - no other options.<br /><br />The E6 is a much more versatile device. It turns on/off via a switch, rather than only by plugging/unplugging your earphones. It has 3 preset EQ settings, which give varying bass, treble or midrange boosts. And it has a volume control. The E3 is useful in boosting bass response if your MP3 player is rather flat sounding - or in boosting volume if your MP3 player just doesn't have enough oomph.<br /><br />Today, I gave the E6 a good workout on a 100-mile ride. Results were positive.<br /><br />First and foremost, it fattened up the sound nicely. The MP3 player built into my Garmin GPS is good, but has no tone controls; sound is very flat and thin. The E6 nicely beefs up the bass. Actually,I tested by riding 50 miles each with two different headphones. The Sonys benefited from additional bottom end and a little volume boost, and the Sennheisers worked well with a middle setting that helped midrange.<br /><br />Second, the E6 definitely provides a solid volume boost, plenty enough to get the volume uncomfortably high if I so desired. Which I didn't; I had to back the volume off quite a bit with the Sennheiser headphones. More important, there was plenty enough volume to listen to a Beethoven concerto at 70mph,so my traveling music no longer has to be restricted to the loudest classic rock albums I can find.<br /><br />Finally, the E6 is surprisingly quiet. In other words, very little hum. The E3 is electronically very noisy. While there is a slight hum from the E6, it isn't noticeable once the music gets going - and certainly not over ambient 70mph bike noise.<br /><br />Also, size matters - or lack of size in this case. Looking at the picture, you might think the Fiio E6 is the size of an IPod. Not so. It is actually about an inch square, 1/4 inch thick, and weighs practically nothing. So whether you keep it stuffed in your pocket or hanging from a tank bag, it isn't bothersome.<br /><br />At about $27 and for my needs, the Fiio E6 is a good buy.

1416972242|4.0|All right people... I thought I could stop after City of Bones, but quite obviously you see that I could not. After finishing City of Bones for the second time, I figured I might as well read the rest of the books in the series as City of Lost Souls comes out in roughly a week. I got through City of Ashes much faster than City of Bones and so hopefully my quick reading streak continues and I can finish City of Glass and City of Angels before May 8th. Now, I'm going to write this as spoiler free as humanly possible, so feel safe in reading my review if you are new to The Mortal Instruments series!<br /><br />City of Ashes takes off right where City of Bones leaves us... some big stuff happens... people change...there's fighting? How can I even begin to describe this book without any spoilers? Well, here goes! Valentine is still bad, very bad. Clary and Jace learn some pretty interesting things about their heritage...think Starwars. Simon and Alec discover whole new lifestyles, and it's definitely not what you think. In this second installment, Clary and the gang must work even harder to overcome not only the bumps that Valentine puts in the road, but they must also overcome a few personal obstacles before they can hope to beat Valentine.<br /><br />In book two, Clare introduces us to her brand of writing where everyone has a part. We've got vampires, werewolves, some man on man love, warlocks, demons, Shadowhunters, Faeries and more. It seems that Cassandra Clare's invisible world is a world just like our own. One full of not only prejudiced people, but all kinds of people. It's not often we see young adult fiction featuring homosexuality, especially in a book series as widely popular as The Mortal Instruments. This is a huge reason for my love of Clare's books: she writes for everyone. Instead of devastatingly beautiful princesses falling in love with dashingly charming princes living happily ever after, Clare's characters are ordinary. Ordinary you ask? Didn't your list above include supernatural demon hunters descended from Angels? Her characters are ordinary in that they have real problems. Simon is in love with his best friend who won't return his love. Clary, while pretty in her own right, is described as an awkward ginger who is short and gangly. We have Shadowhunters finding out how to express to their friends and family that they are gay, in a world where being a warrior and being gay never go hand in hand. The characters go through they own struggles while still saving the day and that is a huge reason why these books as such a must read.<br /><br />The book as a whole sets a different mood from the first book. In City of Bones, we find ourselves dying for as much information as we can, learning about the invisible world in Clare's writing. In this book, we dive further and further into their world with Clary as she discovers more about herself in the mundane world and the Shadowhunter world. She becomes ever stronger as she learns of unrequited love, forbidden love and the love and bonds of a family. Clary grows up fast in these books and unlike many other stories, her growing pains are pains indeed. She comes to realize that life isn't easy, it's the difficult choies that you make that make you an adult, not the fact that you're glowingly happy all the time. Clary is definitely one of my favorites. Dare I say a close second to Hermione Granger... yep I said it.<br /><br />While there is still a lot of character development in this book, we find more in the plot department as well. We learn more of Valentine and his plans and also about the fate of the Clave and the Shadowhunter world. The action in this book is simply awesome as well. While these books do have a fair amount of blood and guts, it's not in excess and even the most squeamish reader will find themselves devouring page after page, yearning for more.<br /><br />What can I say? Each book gets  better and better! B0071XO8RA|3.0|Dear Mr. Howey,<br /><br />Wool is startling, hyponotizing, and audacious. The layered plot, character development and world creation display the high quality of your writing skills. I even like the underlying moral that weaves throughout the story: we can't change what's gone before; we are responsible for what we do now. However, your world view leaves me cold.<br /><br />A sentence in the product description says, \\"But there are always those who hope, who dream.\\" I found no hope in Wool. Under the lies of the ruling elite, \\"life is hard and then you die.\\" What changes when the new leaders tell the truth? In the world you created, the truth changes nothing. Even with the lies exposed, \\"life is hard and then you die.\\" In fact with a logical extrapolation from the ground work you laid in the first five pieces, life could get significantly worse when the truth is told.<br /><br />I believe Wool, as a book and as a series, has the potential to sell well. In my opinion, however, it does not have the appeal to last, to be read, reread, and savored. Madelein l'Engle in Walking on Water expresses precisely what I believe to be missing in Wool. She states, \\"We don't want to feel less when we have finished reading a book; we want to feel that new possibilities of being have opened up to us. We don't want to close a book with a sense that life is totally unfair and that there is no light in the darkness; we want to feel that we have been given illumination.\\" Yet when I finished reading Wool, I felt the opposite of illuminated and enriched; I felt robbed of hope.<br /><br />I'm not asking for \\"happily ever after.\\" I ask simply that change do more than present a new version of the circumstances and that new version without hope of improvement.<br /><br />By-the-way, with my limited experience of a grain elevator (silo) in West Texas, I recall that the problem was not rotting grain. The problem was the high risk of a grain dust explosion killing everyone who worked there. Not a problem, just wasn't expected.<br /><br />Included in the package are a few pieces:<br /><br />The main vest/harness piece (includes the 2 sides + back)<br />2 additional nylon strips w/ hooks<br />Grip for the back<br /><br />Looking at the piece, I was a bit confused on what everything was for. This attached to the top of the back and connects the harness to a backpack, giving you an easy grip to carry both the backpack + harness together. I'm glad they made this (and the 2 optional hooks) optional for those who are not using the backpack.. it's also nice that they included them instead of selling them separately!<br /><br />Finally, there is a strap across the front of the harness which connects the 2 sides together. This strap can be adjusted to whatever you find to be a comfortable position. The grey buckle on it also doubles as a \\"safety whistle\\" and works well. This strap is removable if you don't want it and includes a stretchy portion which is nice (so you don't need to keep it loose all the time - it will just stretch when needed)<br /><br />The 2 straps in front are adjustable to a pretty far length (I found this kind of weird since the strap in back is at a fixed, shorter length. The 2 straps in front also have a cover which keeps them neat and stops them from dangling. For some reason, the strap across the center does not have this \\"cover\\" to keep the excess nylon from hanging.<br /><br />Overall a very nice accessory for anyone using the belt. 0812550706|5.0|i think 'ender's game' is the only book i've read three times. for me books often don't have repeat reading value in the same way some movies have repeat viewing value.  it's probably because a movie takes two hours of your time while a novel, for me, takes a week or longer.  so for someone like to me read a novel twice, not to mention three times, is really saying something [and yes, i realize the inherent snobbery in that statement].<br /><br />i've thought long and hard about what makes 'ender's game' so appealing.  it's got a sympathetic protagonist, lots of great action, lots of heart, and a plausible twist of an ending.  on those merits only 'ender's game' works. it's a lot of fun to read and orson scott card manages to inject some really moral and ethical quandries without resorting to didactism or heavy-handedness.  for example, the manipulations of the battle school powers-that-be are presented and inspected, but card never explicitly paints them as the enemy.  they are who they are, for better or for worse, but it's up to the reader to for his or her own opinions. same for ender and his merry band of castoffs. card understands that good v. bad is never as simple as black v. white.  the world and universe are, more often than not, varying shades of gray. and the folks who inhabit that gray universe, for better or for worse, are who they are. they all have a part, they all have a purpose--even if those parts and purposes contradict each other.<br /><br />'ender's game' is also a great story of the value and importance of friendship. i choke up everytime ender's friends great him over the headset and the kids prepare for the final 'battle.'  who wouldn't want friends like bean, petra, hot soup and the rest? i sure would.<br /><br />but i think the real appeal for 'ender's game' comes from the belief that we all want to believe that there's something uniquely special about us. i think it's safe to assume that most of us have, at one point or another, felt like the underdog, the castoff, the misfit, the misunderstood, or the underappreciated, and that if people would just give us a chance, we'd shine.  in that way ender is very much a universal character.  he embodies a small part of each us.  yes, he is treated unfairly and manipulated, but he's also the smartest kid in the room.  there's something very appealing about that.  at least there is for me.  whether or not i'm the smartest person in the room is irrelevant, but i want to believe it. and whenever i read 'ender's game' there's a small hope that it just might be true.the underdog, the castoff, the misfit, the misunderstood, or the underappreciated, and that if people would just give us a chance, we'd shine.  in that way ender is very much a universal character.  he embodies a small part of each us.  yes, he is treated unfairly and manipulated, but he's also the smartest kid in the room.  there's something very appealing about that.  at least there is for me.  whether or not i'm the smartest person in the room is irrelevant, but i want to believe it. and whenever i read 'ender's game' there's a small hope that it just might be true.

B007JQLNXG|5.0|Just when you think you have Peter Gabriel all figured out, he surprises his fans with yet another innovative approach to musical performance. This time, it's with his new live CD entitled Live Blood. Recorded at the beautifully simple and iconic Hammersmith Apollo in London in March of last year, the acoustics of the venue positively adds to the music that you didn't think could be augmented.<br /><br />Forgoing drums and guitars of a usual rock show - even a progressive rock show like Gabriel's, the distinguished artist instead implemented the 46-piece New Blood Orchestra. In doing so, he \\"re-imagined\\" some of his best known songs with awe-inspiring orchestral arrangements.<br /><br />Picking the Boomerocity short list of favorites is difficult.  In the battle for first is Gabriel's treatment of Paul Simon's The Boy in the Bubble.  As he did on the Scratch My Back album, Peter dials this back a few beats per second as compared to Simon's original.  In a live setting with full orchestra, it really does have a spine tingling effect.<br /><br />Coincidentally, a second choice for our short list is another tune Gabriel recorded on Scratch My Back - The Power of the Heart, which was written by Lou Reed.  As with Bubble, this live version gives chills to the body and leaves your mouth open from its beauty.<br /><br />Last but certainly not least on the Boomerocity short list of favorites is Red Rain which comes from Peter's chart topping 1986 album, So.  Just as the 3D video of this tune is mind-blowing, just listening to the audio is an incredible experience in and of itself.  Add to that experience by listening through a great pair of headphones and you're in for an unforgettable experience.<br /><br />Fans of prog, Gabriel and earlier Genesis will definitely want this CD in their listening library. You won't be disappointed.<br /><br />Boomerocity.com

0307888908|4.0|This book begins with an excellent thought from the Noble Prize winner Mohammad Yunus, \\"As humans we are all born entrepreneurs. When we were in caves, we were all self-employed, finding our own food, feeding ourselves. As civilization came, we suppressed it. We became \\"Labor\\" because they stamped us, \\"You are Labor\\". We forgot that we are entrepreneurs\\". Authored by co-founder of LinkedIn Reid Hoffman and Ben Casnocha a renowned entrepreneur, this book speaks about how one can go about enriching his/her carrier by thinking on the lines of an entrepreneur.<br /><br />The book to me is mainly about the following \\"3\\" things:<br /><br />  How can I stand-out in the competitive world around me?<br />  How do I go about planning to make myself competitive?<br />  Lastly how do I build and develop a give-and-take relationship with the network of people around me?<br /><br />  In the first part, the author explains how we can make ourselves competitive and make ourselves stand-out in the mad rush by focusing and becoming very clear about the following three factors:<br /><br />  Identify what are your \\"assets\\" that make your different or unique from the other individuals in your area of expertise<br />  Identify what are you \\"aspirations\\" or \\"values\\" that guide and motivate your actions<br />  Identify the current \\"market realities\\". Having a skill set which is not needed in the market you are in, will not help you in the long run.<br /><br />Secondly, the author explains why most of us loose our entrepreneurial edge; it is because of a lack of plan. Since we do not have plan in place, we find it very hard to take risks. The author recommends  \\"ABZ\\" planning philosophy.<br /><br />  A: Your Plan-A is your primary plan, which you want to work on or achieve. for e.g. starting a new for-profit business<br />  B: Your Plan-B is the immediate back-up plan for Plan-A. It is always somewhere in the vicinity of Plan-A and not too far away or out of the domain of Plan-A. for e.g. Imight not be able to start my own business, but I might be able to score an executive position in a new start-up<br />  Z: Your Plan-Z is your failure plan. If Plans A & B fail, you should have a Plan-Z that you can go back to. Risks, which lead you to streets are unplanned risks and should never be taken. So in our e.g. your Plan-Z might be to take a contract job if your plans A & B fail<br /><br />In the Last part of the book, the author has gone at length to explain the value of having a network of people around you. In a way, I could sense the author was advertising LinkedIn, but he did it very smartly and explained the advantages of having a good network of people around you. The author points a research which had concluded that every person on this earth, is connected to every other person by an average of \\"7\\" links. So if I am sitting in New Jersey as I type this blog, I am connected to someone in Jakarta by an average of \\"7\\" people. Amazing!<br /><br />Overall this book is an ace for me; it brings to light how we can start thinking like entrepreneurs by developing our competitive advantage, doing the ABZ planning, pursuing breakout opportunities, taking intelligent risks and tapping into the intelligence network around us. I hope I have intrigued you enough, to go read the book.

He references really minor characters from the previous books without giving you any jog of memory as to how you remember them. Of course this can be really annoying and over done in some books, especially if you are powering through a series without stopping, but you have to expect a least a little explanation for minor characters that are brought up again. A lot of times I was just confused, and that is probably why it felt like such a chore to read.<br /><br />Also, it seemed like everything was drawn out in the chapters until a final peak at the end to keep you interested in maintaining that story line until you came across it again later in the book. It's like he had bullet points of the significant event that were suppose to happen in each chapter, and then just wrote fluff around it. I thought the fourth book was a little boring, but the fifth was worse. It also really seemed to 'borrow' a lot of elements of Robert Jordan's book, but maybe I only just noticed this because I began reading his series before getting to the fifth book. Finally, I remember some of the violence and sex scenes adding more intrigue to the previous books, but in the fifth, they just bordered on vulgar. All in all, I doubt I will read this book again or any of them afterwards. I would rather cherish my memory of an awesome series that was the best kept-secret until its exploitation by HBO.

Overall, the structure of the book is very conducive for a step-by-step evaluation of one's career and id potential specific actions to address any gaps or accentuate strengths (some of them seem obvious after reading them - but provides a very good checklist).  In addition to [[ASIN:1591844096 Linchpin: Are You Indispensable?]], this book can become an excellent resource for re-evaluating one's career and define a \\"change plan\\".  The constant comparison (forced) with entrepreneurship distracts the reader primarily because the similarities exist only superficially - the risk profile borne by an individual in these two contexts are so different making any comparisons incomplete. An entrepreneur may also have the luxury to manage risk with developing a portfolio of ideas - far less feasible for an individual with a day job. Despite these minor quibbles, the authors provide a very systematic thought exercise that will benefit anyone looking to reassess career paths. 4.5*

B006AX6ONI|3.0|The thing I liked best about this book was the low price (2.99). The book is a collection of the author's blog posts, but reads quite well, and does not feel totally disjointed as some blog-collections do.<br /><br />The book recounts the author's career in business, and includes the long story of his many failures, dumb things done, and many \\"career\\" switches he made along the way to his eventual success. I couldn't help but feel that his early business life especially seemed to be mostly a long, random, unplanned, party-filled sequence of \\"have fun at whatever seems to be working at the moment\\" events.<br /><br />Nonetheless, he does communicate the message that it wasn't all partying, and that he did in fact work really hard and stay up late to read all those early DOS manuals at the birth of the PC industry, and did in fact catch the PC business wave with his MicroSolutions service company, and did in fact provide a valued business service to real customers, and was in fact able to sell out (maybe near the peak) for enough money to retire and tour the world, as many entrepreneurs did in those days. And you must respect that effort, for sure.<br /><br />FWIW, I would guess this is the journey--running and scaling up a real business -- where he learned a lot of his good business lessons. He doesn't say much at all about selling off Broadcast.com for a stunning pile of money right at the peak of the dot com bubble, which is probably good for this book, because obviously that success had far more to do with being lucky with a willing purchaser at the right time and place in the dot com world of the day than it did with business skill. (Although once again, you must respect the ability to cash in at the right moment, since many did not. Credit to the author for his sales skill and developed senses and timing on that one!)<br /><br />The part of the book I liked the best was at the end, when he gives two short lists of rules and principles that condense his experiences into a consumable form. Some he used for many years in his journey (and so are time-worn and tried and true presumably), but some have the feel of being formed in retrospect, after his successes (which may or may not contribute to their values as \\"how to win\\" or \\"lessons learned in retrospect\\" principles). Anyhow, they are clearly presented and each have enough substance to be worthwhile (as opposed to the shallow and trivial ones you sometimes read in really, really light business books).<br /><br />All in all, I think this book is a good buy because of its price, but I wouldn't feel good about paying $15, or even $10 for it, because I couldn't find anything new (to me) in it. But for the price, it was a reasonable story and affirmation of some useful principles for running your life.<br /><br />Come to think of it, I would definitely say that his principles for running your life (and esp for people of college or college grad age) are the best part of the book, by far. And I find it funny-haha (not funny strange) how I come away smiling at the thought of the author writing mostly to college-age party kids when he's travelled a unique (and often party-filled) path to massive fortune by catching the once-in-a-lifetime dot-com wave and playing it masterfully with a really big dose of luck at the blow off top. Who knows, maybe you can party lots and still be massively successful if you're in the right place at the right time, catch the wave, and don't screw up too badly, so you can ride it to the top and sell out before the crash.<br /><br />My last comments remind me to recommend that you go read Gladwell's Outliers book, where he argues very well that Gates and Jobs were also exactly the right age, and exactly in the right place and time to also catch the PC wave that made them rich. That was a very interesting argument, because I was there (as a kid) reading about the start of it all in the late 60s, and Gladwell makes a very good case that if Gates and Jobs wereeven a few year older or younger, they would have missed their particular waves, and the successes would have gone to other people.<br /><br />Also when I think of partying and success and Cuban, I think of Branson -- I should go read a story or two on him too... I love biographies, no question.

1400069289|5.0|It is said that we are all creatures of habit, and if there was any doubt that's true, Charles Duhigg's book is all the proof you will need.  His idea is simple; our lives are made up of all of our little habits, from brushing our teeth to not going to the gym in the morning.  We do the things we do because we have trained ourselves to behave that way, unthinking in our actions. Once you've done the same thing a number of times your brain becomes wired to follow the same path.  However, we are not bound by these habits, and difficult thought it may be, we can change our habits. It's all about the cycles of cue (what sets a habit in motion), routine (what you do when the cue occurs), and reward (the expected satisfaction you get when you've completed the routine).  By recognizing this pattern and altering it to get the desired result we change our habits.  The process isn't easy, and it will take effort, but knowing is half the battle, and once you start repeating your desired action enough a new, and better, habit will be formed.<br /><br />The simplistic summary above just begins to touch the surface of Mr. Duhigg's powerful and interesting book.  The book covers not only the habits of individuals, but also of organizations, and society.  There are many fascinating studies, stories, and examples of successfully changed habits both large and small in the book to illustrate all of his points.  The section that deals with how our minds work and are wired, was especially engaging to me.<br /><br />Everything is explained in plain language, so you don't have to be a scientist to follow it.  Better still, lots of great illustrations are scattered through the book, to assist in the understanding of the concepts.<br /><br />All in all...a fun and fascinating read, that can also change your life.

Have a great cause<br /><br />By being a technology expert, Guy is heavily invested in social media and describes in detail how to leverage new media to your advantage. The topics of \\"likeability\\" and \\"trust\\" have been written on ad nauseum, and there really is little need for Guy to explain, for example, how to shake hands correctly.<br /><br />In the end, it is worth the read, but not nearly as \\"enchanting\\" as I would have hoped.

This book makes you think of the motions that love makes you do. The term lovesick comes to mind. And yes love can make you do crazy things. Have crazy thoughts. I've always been a strong believer that you are allowed to have whatever feelings you desire, for it's your actions that you are accounted for. It how you handle your feelings. That's what matters.<br /><br />The main character in the series is a 17 year old girl named Lena who is just a few months shy of receiving the procedure. When we first meet Lena she is pretty much counting down the days until her procedure. She has had some pretty hardships in her life. Her father died of cancer when she was very young, and her mother committed suicide after having 3 failed procedures for the cure. The cure is what they call the love removing procedure. Lena was raised by her aunt Carol, who was her mother's sister.<br /><br />If I lost the ability to love, there is no doubt I'd be a different person. A mother's love is one the most precious things. The idea of raising children without love frightens me. It's interesting to see how Lena's aunt who is cured treats Lena and her own children. Lena's younger cousin Grace does not speak at all. Carol pretty much has cast Grace aside and just tells everyone that she's dumb. It's clear that the lack of love in her life is one of the factors in Grace's behavior.<br /><br />Most dystopian novels deal with the actions of the government, and Delirium makes me wonder how far the government could realistically make things. In Delirium big brother is pretty much watching you at all times. The government decides everything for you. From your job to who you are paired to marry. Marriage pairings remind me of arranged marriages. I despise arrange marriages. No freedom. No choice. I'm a romantic. I personally could never live in this world.<br /><br />Very early on in the book Lena meets Alex. From the first moment they meet, she is pretty much intrigued by him. Lena has not had much contact with boys because the government forbids uncured males and females from talking to each other outside of their family. Boys and girls go to separate schools. Alex pretty much captivates Lena. I believe a part of it is the fact that she knows that what she's doing is considered to be wrong. But mostly I think it's just a part of growing up. It reminded me of the feelings you have for your first crush. When you realize that boys aren't icky and don't have cooties. When Lena meets a transformation starts to happen. The reader really gets to see her start to grow into a women.<br /><br />One of the things I enjoyed the most about this book was the steady flow. Everything went at a nice pace, and was really well written. I didn't find any parts to drag on, or move too quickly. Lena's transformation was pretty gradual and it was a joy to read. The climax of the book had my heart racing. I am also extremely glad that the next book in the series Pandemonium is already out. I cannot wait to read it.rry. 0525478817|5.0|I'm going to keep this rather short and simple as my back is killing me and I need to lay down, but having just finished the book, I am obligated towards recommending it. I'm not going to summarize the story as that's in the description, you're able to read the first chapter on Amazon for free, John promotes the book above, and, of course, the many readers here have already reviewed and laid it out for you. The progression of this book was so well-done, that I couldn't put it down. Heck, I took it with me to class to read this morning (bad, I know, but this professor is completely book-oriented and I'm just there for the attendance). I immediately took a liking to most of the characters introduced, appreciating their knowledge and backstory to their way of speaking or expressing themselves. With quite a few books I've come across, the author tends to \\"show off\\" his/her vocabulary, and they have every right to, they've earned their publication, their hard work, their knowledge on the language being quite impressive. But with that, it's difficult to imagine how...in reality, a boy of poverty struggling to get by to speak so adequately. If there is a believable way to explain such grammar, then I'm all ears, or in this case, all eyes. Greene accomplishes this, as we get a good sense of background and standing with the characters. I know this seems like a minor quirk with books being narrated as such, but nevertheless, I feel that I must point it out.<br /><br />Anyway, the character development, particularly of the main character, Hazel, has truly touched me. Her struggle, her rather realistic views, her ways of coping with her complications, it's all so inspiring. And not the \\"inspiring\\" in a sense that, \\"hey, this girl is strong and living and getting by, battling cancer and everything!\\" but more-so in the sense that the book, or rather, Hazel's and a few other characters, see themselves and see the world, no, the universe, in such a substantial and unique way. Theirpaths, their lives, takes many turns, each with valuable lessons and growth. In short, I thoroughly enjoyed how \\"real\\" these characters felt.<br /><br />This book has reminded me just how precious life is. You hear it all the time, \\"you don't know what you have until you've lost it,\\" \\"here today, gone tomorrow,\\" or \\"I won't be around forever,\\" things loved ones have said but weren't taken to heart as we neglect and deny the thoughts of them -really and truly- leaving us. But no, we can't stay in that cozy dark cave forever. We have to wake up and realize the truth behind these statements, and cherish our moments together even more than before. I believe our memories can keep us going upon losing that special someone, as well as the support of family and friends. On a more personal note relating to this realization, I'm currently facing quite a few complications in my life. Almost hitting the big 2-5, I've began getting rather sick late last year, beginning with rather simple but annoying migraines day-by-day, and now escalating into abdominal pains, nausea, dizziness, sensitivity to light, so on and so forth. It's the nausea and abdominal pains that really keep me leveled, it keeps me from activities and I hate that. Before the end of last year, I was able to get up, get set, and get out the house with ease. But now, now I feel like hell some mornings that I just don't have the motivation to go somewhere I'm invited to. But...if characters such as Hazel and Augustus and Isaac can make this effort, if they can keep going, and they've certainly had it worse than me, then I sure as hell will try to, too. It's worth going to that rather boring seminar with a relative, worth heading to a friend's small business opening, worth it all. As unsociable as I am, I now more than ever want to make the effort to at least show up and be there for others, spend that precious little time that we have. I don't prefer to be close with many, I like my small circle just the way it is,but it's as psychoanalyst Harry Stack Sullivan said, \\"loneliness is the most painful human experience.\\" And damn it, it is.<br /><br />In any case, I thank you for reading my attempted recommendation of this book. Please, you owe it to yourself to give it a read.<br /><br />*Edit*<br />Looks like it's getting a movie!

B00005JLIU|2.0|Thoroughly Moronic Millie<br /><br />Wow!  I think that when I'm on my deathbed, my greatest regret will be having forever lost the 2 1/2 hours of my life I squandered watching one of the lamest, botched attempts at musical comedy ever vomited onto the silver screen.  If you should hate your boss so much that you'd like to pull a John Wilkes Booth on him or her, then hand the reprobate a copy of this 1920s satire and glow about how great you thought it was.  He or she should have hung him- or herself in their garage by the end of the first act.<br /><br />Blake Edwards was a first-class director and always got the best performance from his actors, but he often had a knack for creating comedies that were so dry they made Death Valley look like the Pacific Ocean.  The original Arthur was one of his rare exceptions.<br /><br />There was terrible miscasting to contend with as well.  Although Julie Andrews gives a knockout performance and carries the movie, she is a bad choice and likely got the role for being Blake Edwards' wife.  No one has ever really justified how her aristocratic London accent makes sense here, given that her role is that of a flunky secretary who wants to find a rich boss to marry for financial security.  There's nothing in the plot that elucidates why she's the only British person in the movie.  How much sense would it make for William F. Buckley to play the role of Rocky Balboa instead of Sylvester Stallone?  Or maybe one of the Wayans brothers as Hamlet?  I rest my case.<br /><br />And pointing out that this is a tongue-in-cheek, campy little romp doesn't cut it:  characters still have to be appropriate to their roles for credibility's sake.  And though Mary Tyler Moore looks the part of a naive ingenue, she mostly comes off as a pasty-faced waif whose performance is more wooden than Roger Moore's during his turn as James Bond.  Only Carol Channing fits her role as the kooky swell with a heart of gold.<br /><br />How this movie got rated as a musical escapes me, given that it had very few songs, only a few of which were actually performed. Most were just background pieces.  This was a movie with music, period.  And for what it's worth, the music was about the only redeeming aspect of this celluloid dung heap.  Elmer Bernstein's score garnered an Oscar, and deservedly so:  it was a spot-on pastiche of 20s music that was relentlessly joyful and splashy.  Its inspired flair was instrumental in giving this movie a sense of pacing or momentum.  Also of great merit was the venerable Cahn and Van Heusen title song, Thoroughly Modern Millie, which well-deserved its Oscar nomination.  This legendary team, who were a favorite song source for Frank Sinatra, produced a masterful flapper-era story song that was a lyrical tour-de-force, and serves as a lesson for anyone interested in becoming a legitimate song writer.<br /><br />But if you want to see what a 20s musical comedy actually looks like, rent The Marx Brother's hilarious The Cocoanuts or Cole Porter's wacky Anything Goes or whimsical Les Girls.  The only thing funny about Thoroughly Modern Millie are the moments when Julie Andrews mugs for the camera, but in her case it was merely cute for mostly looking staged, whereas when Groucho Marx did it in his movies, it was actually funny, and largely because it looked genuinely spontaneous.<br /><br />What blows my mind is that this travesty was eventually adapted as a Broadway musical.  Really?  Its plot line is so gossamer-thin, that if it were a pizza crust it would have stretched out far enough to cover Texas.  \\"Millie\\" qualifies as a \\"popcorn\\" musical at best.  But for contemporary musical stage fare with a 20s motif, you're much better off buying a ticket to The Drowsy Chaperone, which is just as lightweight, but exponentially more fun.<br /><br />I'd like to rant on further about how thoroughly ripped off I felt by the end of Thoroughly Modern Millie, but I've just hung myself in my garage and I can't reach mycomputer keyboard from up here.

This way when such production devices became important, the viewer had a trained eye--not unlike what was done with White Noise. These movies convey a style that is very unusual. So it came as no surprise to me that there was little middle ground in people's opinions of them; they loved'em or hated'em. I love'em. Why? Because my senses are all on full power; I'm all in; I'm practically concentrating on the screen and listening to every creak trying to sleuth out the next clue that something fishy is going on in that house. Some people may call this \\"work\\". I call it cool. [But people chatting during the movie are a much greater distraction than normal under the circumstances.]<br /><br />This third installment was my least favorite, but I can totally understand why it may be others' favorite. While it still employed subtle moving objects, it did it less, instead relying on more mainstream devices to provoke scared jumps from the audience. It also borrowed more heavily from the Poltergeist movies than the first two--not that I minded. The characters' investigation into the strange goings-on was more methodical and plot-driven. The first two were more event-driven and investigated more out of fear and curiosity. The differences between 3 and 1 & 2 were subtle, but numerous, chief amongst them being that \\"the paranormal\\" functions as a character in this movie, rather than a mysterious \\"force\\" in the first two. As such, the actions of \\"the paranormal\\" were more blatant and felt more like \\"it\\" was doing something \\"to someone\\" whereas in 1 & 2 it was more like \\"something was happening\\" to someone in a haunted house.<br /><br />The plot is simple. A family lives in a house. Weird things start happening. Step-dad starts putting cameras around the house. Things get weirder. And from there anything more would give too much away. But while the first two movies' endings left us largely to wild speculation, I will say that this one ends with something of an explanation of the paranormal activity.<br /><br />It doesn't really matter in which order you watch these movies. However, each sequel (or in this case prequel) was designed to mold over the events of the previous installment. So watch them in order if it's convenient. I also demand you watch these with the lights off. It makes it easier to avoid distractions while you're concentrating on the screen.family lives in a house. Weird things start happening. Adiga's novel begins with an address to `The Premier's Office in Beijing, Capital of the Freedom-Loving Nation of China' given by the son of a rickshaw-puller, Balram Halwai. Balram, our narrator, is a wanted man, having, as he soon tells us, cut the throat of his employer, Mr Ashok.  Balram was his driver and had become that very rare thing, a trusted servant, in his employer's eyes.  Gradually we learn of the seedy world behind the fine promises of democracy; we learn of the way that the ever more expanding and ever more desperate poor are treated by the rich and powerful, of the hypocrisy and indifference of the rich few to the plight of the teeming millions whom they exploit.  And in this book it is funny - for we readers, that is.<br /><br />Does Balram exaggerate?  Possibly, but the reader is entertained by the antics of this `entrepreneur' who is forced to become, and happy to call himself, `a mass murderer.' True he has only killed one man, but his example will likely be followed by other slaves who are obliged to kill in order to survive.
